CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Robert H. Goddard</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Goddard&amp;redirect=no" title="Robert Goddard">Robert Goddard</a>)</span></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other persons with the name Robert Goddard, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Robert_Goddard_(disambiguation)" title="Robert Goddard (disambiguation)">Robert Goddard (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox biography vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><div class="fn" style="display:inline">Robert H. Goddard</div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Robert_H._Goddard_-_GPN-2002-000131.jpg"><img alt="Dr. Robert H. srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Dr._Robert_H._Goddard_-_GPN-2002-000131.jpg/330px-Dr._Robert_H._Goddard_-_GPN-2002-000131.jpg 1.5x, (1882&#8211;1945)</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Born</th><td><div class="nickname" style="display:inline">Robert Hutchings Goddard</div><br/><span style="display:none">(<span Massachusetts</a>, U.S.</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Died</th><td>August 10, 1945<span style="display:none">(1945-08-10)</span> (aged&#160;62)<br/><div class="deathplace" style="display:inline"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore,_Maryland" title="Baltimore, Maryland">Baltimore, Maryland</a>, U.S</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Nationality</th><td class="category">American</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Education</th><td><a href="/wiki/Worcester_Polytechnic_Institute" title="Worcester Polytechnic Institute">Worcester Polytechnic Institute</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Clark_University" title="Clark University">Clark University</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Occupation</th><td class="role"><a href="/wiki/Professor" href="/wiki/Inventor" title="Inventor">inventor</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Known&#160;for</th><td>First <a href="/wiki/Liquid-propellant_rocket" title="Liquid-propellant rocket">liquid-fueled</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><span class="nowrap">Spouse(s)</span></th><td><div style="position:relative;display:inline-block;line-height:normal;">Esther Christine Kisk (<abbr title="married">m.</abbr>&#160;1924&#8211;1945)</div> (1901&#8211;1982)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Awards</th><td><a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a> <small>(1959)</small><br/><a href="/wiki/Langley_Gold_Medal" title="Langley Gold Medal">Langley Gold Medal</a> <small>(1960)</small><br/><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Guggenheim_Medal" title="Daniel Guggenheim Medal">Daniel Guggenheim Medal</a> <small>(1964)</small></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Robert Hutchings Goddard</b> (October&#160;5, 1882 &#8211; August&#160;10, 1945) was an American <a href="/wiki/Engineer" title="Engineer">engineer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Professor" title="Professor">professor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inventor" title="Inventor">inventor</a> who is credited with creating and building the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Liquid-propellant_rocket" title="Liquid-propellant rocket">liquid-fueled</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Goddard successfully launched his model on March 16, 1926, ushering in an era of space flight and innovation. He and his team launched 34 rockets<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goddard_Launch_History_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goddard_Launch_History-2">[2]</a></sup> between 1926 and 1941, achieving altitudes as high as 2.6&#160;km (1.6&#160;mi) and speeds as fast as 885&#160;km/h (550&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goddard_Launch_History_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goddard_Launch_History-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard\'s work as both theorist and engineer anticipated many of the developments that were to make spaceflight possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunley_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunley-3">[3]</a></sup> He has been called the man who ushered in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Age" title="Space Age">Space Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RockDev_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-RockDev-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>xiii</span></sup> Two of Goddard\'s 214 patented inventions&#8212;a multi-stage rocket (1914), and a liquid-fuel rocket (1914)&#8212;were important milestones toward spaceflight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> His 1919 <a href="/wiki/Monograph" title="Monograph">monograph</a> <i><a href="#A_Method_of_Reaching_Extreme_Altitudes">A Method of Reaching Extreme Altitudes</a></i> is considered one of the classic texts of 20th-century rocket science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Goddard successfully applied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_dynamics_(aircraft)" title="Flight dynamics (aircraft)">three-axis control</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyroscopes" title="Gyroscopes">gyroscopes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thrust_vectoring" title="Thrust vectoring">steerable thrust</a> to rockets to effectively control their flight.\n</p><p>Although his work in the field was revolutionary, Goddard received very little public support for his research and development work. The press sometimes ridiculed his theories of spaceflight. As a result, he became protective of his privacy and his work. Years after his death, at the dawn of the Space Age, he came to be recognized as one of the founding fathers of modern rocketry, along with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Esnault-Pelterie" title="Robert Esnault-Pelterie">Robert Esnault-Pelterie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Tsiolkovsky" title="Konstantin Tsiolkovsky">Konstantin Tsiolkovsky</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Oberth" title="Hermann Oberth">Hermann Oberth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> He not only recognized the potential of rockets for atmospheric research, <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile" title="Ballistic missile">ballistic missiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">space travel</a> but was the first to scientifically study, design and construct the rockets needed to implement those ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LynnJenner_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-LynnJenner-13">[13]</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">Goddard Space Flight Center</a> was named in Goddard\'s honor in 1959.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" class="toctext">Other firsts</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Quotations"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Quotations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Timeline"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Timeline</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_inspiration">Early life and inspiration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and inspiration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Goddard was born in <a href="/wiki/Worcester,_Massachusetts" title="Worcester, Massachusetts">Worcester, Massachusetts</a>, to Nahum Danford Goddard (1859&#8211;1928), a farmer, and Fannie Louise Hoyt (1864&#8211;1920). Robert was their only child to survive; a younger son, Richard Henry, was born with a spinal deformity and died before his first birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>16</span></sup> Goddard\'s family had roots in New England dating to the late 1600s. Shortly after his birth the family moved to Boston. With a curiosity about nature, he studied the heavens using a telescope from his father and observed the birds flying. Essentially a country boy, he loved the outdoors and hiking with his father on trips to Worcester and became an excellent marksman with a rifle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoiko_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stoiko-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>63,64</span></sup> In 1898 his mother contracted tuberculosis and they moved back to Worcester for the clear air. On Sundays the family attended the Episcopal church, and Robert sang in the choir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>16</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_experiment">Childhood experiment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Childhood experiment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>With the <a href="/wiki/Electrification" title="Electrification">electrification</a> of American cities in the 1880s, the young Goddard became interested in science&#8212;specifically, engineering and technology. When his father showed him how to generate static electricity on the family\'s carpet, the five-year-old\'s imagination was sparked. Robert experimented, believing he could jump higher if the <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> from a battery could be charged by scuffing his feet on the gravel walk. But, holding the zinc, he could jump no higher than usual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>15</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-58602-966-4_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-58602-966-4-16">[16]</a></sup> Goddard halted the experiments after a warning from his mother that if he succeeded, he could "go sailing away and might not be able to come back."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>9</span></sup>\nHe experimented with chemicals and created a cloud of smoke and an explosion in the house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoiko_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stoiko-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>64</span></sup>\nGoddard\'s father further encouraged Robert\'s scientific interest by providing him with a telescope, a microscope, and a subscription to <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>10</span></sup> Robert developed a fascination with flight, first with <a href="/wiki/Kite" title="Kite">kites</a> and then with <a href="/wiki/Balloon" title="Balloon">balloons</a>. He became a thorough diarist and documenter of his work&#8212;a skill that would greatly benefit his later career. These interests merged at age 16, when Goddard attempted to construct a balloon out of aluminum, shaping the raw metal in his home workshop, and filling it with hydrogen. After nearly five weeks of methodical, documented efforts, he finally abandoned the project, remarking, "... balloon will not go up. ... Aluminum is too heavy. Failior  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] crowns enterprise." However, the lesson of this failure did not restrain Goddard\'s growing determination and confidence in his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>21</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cherry_tree_dream">Cherry tree dream</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cherry tree dream">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>He became interested in space when he read <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>\' science fiction classic <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i> at 16 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> His dedication to pursuing space flight became fixed on October 19, 1899. The 17-year-old Goddard climbed a cherry tree to cut off dead limbs. He was transfixed by the sky, and his imagination grew. He later wrote:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>On this day I climbed a tall cherry tree at the back of the barn ... and as I looked toward the fields at the east, I imagined how wonderful it would be to make some device which had even the <i>possibility</i> of ascending to Mars, and how it would look on a small scale, if sent up from the meadow at my feet. I have several photographs of the tree, taken since, with the little ladder I made to climb it, leaning against it.\n</p><p>It seemed to me then that a weight whirling around a horizontal shaft, moving more rapidly above than below, could furnish lift by virtue of the greater centrifugal force at the top of the path.\n</p><p>I was a different boy when I descended the tree from when I ascended. Existence at last seemed very purposive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>26</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Goddard_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Goddard-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>For the rest of his life he observed October 19 as "Anniversary Day", a private commemoration of the day of his greatest inspiration.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_early_studies">Education and early studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Education and early studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It has often proved true that the dream of yesterday is the hope of today and the reality of tomorrow.</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite>Robert Goddard, <i>On Taking Things for Granted</i>, 1904.</cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The young Goddard was a thin and frail boy, almost always in fragile health. He suffered from stomach problems, pleurisy, colds and bronchitis, and fell two years behind his classmates. He became a voracious reader, regularly visiting the local public library to borrow books on the physical sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>16,19</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerodynamics_and_motion">Aerodynamics and motion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Aerodynamics and motion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Goddard\'s interest in aerodynamics led him to study some of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_Langley" title="Samuel Langley">Samuel Langley</a>\'s scientific papers in the periodical <i><a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_(magazine)" title="Smithsonian (magazine)">Smithsonian</a></i>. In these papers, Langley wrote that birds flap their wings with different force on each side to turn in the air. Inspired by these articles, the teenage Goddard watched swallows and chimney swifts from the porch of his home, noting how subtly the birds moved their wings to control their flight. He noted how remarkably the birds controlled their flight with their tail feathers, which he called the birds\' equivalent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ailerons" title="Ailerons">ailerons</a>. He took exception to some of Langley\'s conclusions and in 1901 wrote a letter to <i>St. Nicholas</i> magazine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5</span></sup> with his own ideas. The editor of  <i>St. Nicholas</i> declined to publish Goddard\'s letter, remarking that birds fly with a certain amount of intelligence and that "machines will not act with such intelligence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>31</span></sup> Goddard disagreed, believing that a man could control a flying machine with his own intelligence.\n</p><p>Around this time, Goddard read <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton\'s</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi&#230; Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i>, and found that <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton\'s laws of motion">Newton\'s Third Law of Motion</a> applied to motion in space. He wrote later about his own tests of the Law:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I began to realize that there might be something after all to Newton\'s Laws. The Third Law was accordingly tested, both with devices suspended by rubber bands and by devices on floats, in the little brook back of the barn, and the said law was verified conclusively. It made me realize that if a way to navigate space were to be discovered, or invented, it would be the result of a knowledge of physics and mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>32</span></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As his health improved, Goddard continued his formal schooling as a 19-year-old sophomore at <a href="/wiki/South_High_Community_School" title="South High Community School">South High Community School</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> in Worcester in 1901. He excelled in his coursework, and his peers twice elected him class president. Making up for lost time, he studied books on mathematics, astronomy, mechanics and composition from the school library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>32</span></sup> At his graduation ceremony in 1904, he gave his class oration as <a href="/wiki/Valedictorian" title="Valedictorian">valedictorian</a>. In his speech, entitled "On Taking Things for Granted", Goddard included a section that would become emblematic of his life:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>[J]ust as in the sciences we have learned that we are too ignorant to safely pronounce anything impossible, so for the individual, since we cannot know just what are his limitations, we can hardly say with certainty that anything is necessarily within or beyond his grasp. Each must remember that no one can predict to what heights of wealth, fame, or usefulness he may rise until he has honestly endeavored, and he should derive courage from the fact that all sciences have been, at some time, in the same condition as he, and that it has often proved true that the dream of yesterday is the hope of today and the reality of tomorrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19</span></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Goddard enrolled at <a href="/wiki/Worcester_Polytechnic_Institute" title="Worcester Polytechnic Institute">Worcester Polytechnic Institute</a> in 1904.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-9"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>41</span></sup> He quickly impressed the head of the physics department, A. Wilmer Duff, with his thirst for knowledge, and Duff took him on as a laboratory assistant and tutor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-10"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>42</span></sup> At WPI, Goddard joined the <a href="/wiki/Sigma_Alpha_Epsilon" title="Sigma Alpha Epsilon">Sigma Alpha Epsilon</a> fraternity, and began a long courtship with high school classmate Miriam Olmstead, an honor student who had graduated with him as <a href="/wiki/Salutatorian" title="Salutatorian">salutatorian</a>. Eventually, she and Goddard were engaged, but they drifted apart and ended the engagement around 1909.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-11"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>51</span></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard received his <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Science" title="Bachelor of Science">B.S.</a> degree in physics from Worcester Polytechnic in 1908,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-12"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>50</span></sup> and after serving there for a year as an instructor in physics, he began his graduate studies at <a href="/wiki/Clark_University" title="Clark University">Clark University</a> in Worcester in the fall of 1909.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Goddard received his <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Arts" title="Master of Arts">M.A.</a> degree in physics from Clark University in 1910, and then stayed at Clark to complete his <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Ph.D.</a> in physics in 1911. He spent another year at Clark as an honorary fellow in physics, and in 1912 he accepted a research fellowship at <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Frist_Campus_Center" title="Frist Campus Center">Palmer Physical Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-13"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>56&#8211;58</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_scientific_writings">First scientific writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: First scientific writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The high school student summed up his ideas on space travel in a proposed article, "The Navigation of Space," which he submitted to the <i>Popular Science News</i>. The journal\'s editor returned it, saying that they could not use it "in the near future."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-14"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>34</span></sup>\n</p><p>While still an undergraduate, Goddard wrote a paper proposing a method for balancing aeroplanes using gyro-stabilization. He submitted the idea to <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>, which published the paper in 1907. Goddard later wrote in his diaries that he believed his paper was the first proposal of a way to automatically stabilize aircraft in flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-15"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>50</span></sup> His proposal came around the same time as other scientists were making breakthroughs in developing functional <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a>.\n</p><p>His first writing on the possibility of a liquid-fueled rocket came on February 2, 1909. Goddard had begun to study ways of increasing a rocket\'s efficiency using methods differing from conventional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid-fuel_rocket" title="Solid-fuel rocket">solid-fuel rockets</a>. He wrote in his journal about using liquid hydrogen as a fuel with liquid oxygen as the oxidizer. He believed that 50 percent efficiency could be achieved with these liquid propellants (i.e., half of the heat energy of combustion converted to kinetic energy of the exhaust gases).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-16"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>55</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_patents">First patents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: First patents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In the decades around 1910, radio was a new technology, fertile for innovation. In 1912, while working at Princeton University, Goddard investigated the effects of radio waves on insulators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In order to generate radio-frequency power, he invented a vacuum tube that operated like a cathode-ray oscillator tube. <span><a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US1159209" rel="nofollow">U.S. Patent 1,159,209</a></span> was issued on November 2, 1915. This was the first use of a vacuum tube to amplify a signal, preceding even <a href="/wiki/Lee_de_Forest" title="Lee de Forest">Lee de Forest</a>\'s claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-17"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>55</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1912 he had in his spare time, using calculus, developed the mathematics which allowed him to calculate the position and velocity of a rocket in vertical flight, given the weight of the rocket and weight of the propellant and the velocity (with respect to the rocket frame) of the exhaust gases. His first goal was to build a sounding rocket with which to study the atmosphere. Not only would such investigation aid meteorology, but it was necessary to determine temperature, density and wind speed in order to design efficient space launch vehicles. He was very reluctant to admit that his ultimate goal was in fact to develop a vehicle for flights into space, since most scientists, especially in the United States, did not consider such a goal to be a realistic or practical scientific pursuit, nor was the public yet ready to seriously consider such ideas. Later, in 1933, Goddard said that "[I]n no case must we allow ourselves to be deterred from the achievement of space travel, test by test and step by step, until one day we succeed, cost what it may."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoiko_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stoiko-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>65,67,74,101</span></sup>\n</p><p>Unfortunately, in early 1913, Goddard became seriously ill with <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> and had to leave his position at Princeton. He then returned to Worcester, where he began a prolonged process of recovery. His doctors did not expect him to live. He decided he should spend time outside in the fresh air and walk for exercise, and he gradually improved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-18"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>61&#8211;64</span></sup> When his nurse discovered some of his notes in his bed, he kept them, arguing,"I have to live to do this work."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoiko_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stoiko-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>66</span></sup>\n</p><p>It was during this period of recuperation, however, that Goddard began to produce some of his most important work. As his symptoms subsided, he allowed himself to work an hour per day with his notes made at Princeton. In the technological and manufacturing atmosphere of Worcester, patents were considered essential, not only to protect original work, but as documentation of first discovery. He began to see the importance of his ideas as intellectual property, and thus began to secure those ideas before someone else did&#8212;and he would have to pay to use them. In May 1913, he wrote concerning his first rocket patent applications. His father brought them to a patent lawyer in a firm in Worcester, who helped him to refine his ideas for consideration. Goddard\'s first patent application was submitted in October 1913.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-19"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>63&#8211;70</span></sup>\n</p><p>In 1914, his first two landmark <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patents" title="Patents">patents</a> were accepted and registered. The first, <span><a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US1102653" rel="nofollow">U.S. Patent 1,102,653</a></span>, described a multi-stage rocket fueled with a solid "explosive material." The second, <span><a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US1103503" rel="nofollow">U.S. Patent 1,103,503</a></span>, described a rocket fueled with a <a href="/wiki/Solid_fuel" title="Solid fuel">solid fuel</a> (explosive material) or with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_propellant" title="Liquid propellant">liquid propellants</a> (gasoline and liquid nitrous oxide). The two patents would eventually become important milestones in the history of rocketry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sea_and_Sky:_Space_Exploration_Timeline_1600&#8211;1960_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sea_and_Sky:_Space_Exploration_Timeline_1600&#8211;1960-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milestones_of_space_exploration_in_the_20th_century_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Milestones_of_space_exploration_in_the_20th_century-26">[26]</a></sup> Overall, he published 214 patents, some posthumously by his wife.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_rocketry_research">Early rocketry research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Early rocketry research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:320px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv/320px--Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:320px;height:240px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="107.0069" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/ac/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="303168" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/ac/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="316672" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 320 &#215; 240 (559 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="558636" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/ac/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv.120p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Lowest bandwidth VP9 (120P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 120P" data-transcodekey="120p.vp9.webm" data-width="160" data-height="120" data-bandwidth="167008" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/ac/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="214" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="197896" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/ac/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv.180p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 180P" data-transcodekey="180p.vp9.webm" data-width="240" data-height="180" data-bandwidth="244080" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Robert Goddard footage.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv/220px--Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Goddard_footage.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Video clips of Goddard\'s launches and other events in his life</div></div></div>\n<p>In the fall of 1914, Goddard\'s health had improved, and he accepted a part-time position as an instructor and research fellow at Clark University.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-20"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>73</span></sup> His position at Clark allowed him to further his rocketry research. He ordered numerous supplies that could be used to build rocket prototypes for launch and spent much of 1915 in preparation for his first tests. Goddard\'s first test launch of a powder rocket came on an early evening in 1915 following his daytime classes at Clark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-21"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>74</span></sup> The launch was loud and bright enough to arouse the alarm of the campus janitor, and Goddard had to reassure him that his experiments, while being serious study, were also quite harmless. After this incident, Goddard took his experiments inside the physics lab, in order to limit any disturbance.\n</p><p>At the Clark physics lab, Goddard conducted static tests of powder rockets to measure their thrust and efficiency. He found his earlier estimates to be verified; powder rockets were converting only about 2 percent of their fuel into thrust. At this point he applied <a href="/wiki/De_Laval_nozzle" title="De Laval nozzle">de Laval nozzles</a>, which were generally used with steam turbine engines, and these greatly improved efficiency. (Of the several definitions of rocket efficiency, Goddard measured in his laboratory what is today called the <i>internal efficiency</i> of the engine: the ratio of the kinetic energy of the exhaust gases to the available thermal energy of combustion, expressed as a percentage.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>130</span></sup> By mid-summer of 1915, Goddard had obtained an average efficiency of 40 percent with a nozzle exit velocity of <span class="nowrap">6728 feet (2051 meters) per second</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-22"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>75</span></sup> Connecting a combustion chamber full of gunpowder to various converging-diverging expansion nozzles, Goddard was able in static tests to achieve engine efficiencies of more than 63% and exhaust velocities of over 7000 feet (2134 meters) per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-23"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>78</span></sup> Few would recognize it at the time, but this little engine was a major breakthrough. These experiments suggested that rockets could be made powerful enough to escape Earth and travel into space. This engine, and subsequent experiments sponsored by the Smithsonian Institution, were the beginning of modern rocketry and, ultimately, space exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pendray_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pendray-28">[28]</a></sup> Goddard realized, however, that it would take the more efficient liquid propellants to reach space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gruntman_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gruntman-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later that year, Goddard designed an elaborate experiment at the Clark physics lab and proved that a rocket would perform in a vacuum such as that in space. He believed it would, but many other scientists were not yet convinced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-86341-553-9_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-86341-553-9-30">[30]</a></sup> His experiment demonstrated that a rocket\'s performance actually decreases under atmospheric pressure.\n</p><p>From 1916 to 1917, Goddard built and tested experimental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_thrusters" title="Ion thrusters">ion thrusters</a>, which he thought might be used for <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">propulsion in the near-vacuum conditions of outer space</a>. The small glass engines he built were tested at atmospheric pressure, where they generated a stream of ionized air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smithsonian_Institution_sponsorship">Smithsonian Institution sponsorship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Smithsonian Institution sponsorship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By 1916, the cost of Goddard\'s rocket research had become too great for his modest teaching salary to bear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-24"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>76</span></sup> He began to solicit potential sponsors for financial assistance, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Aero_Club_of_America" title="Aero Club of America">Aero Club of America</a>.\n</p><p>In his letter to the Smithsonian in September 1916, Goddard claimed he had achieved a 63% efficiency and a nozzle velocity of almost <span class="nowrap">2438 meters per second</span>. With these performance levels, he believed a rocket could vertically lift a weight of <span class="nowrap">1 lb (0.45 kg)</span> to a height of <span class="nowrap"> 232 miles (373 km)</span> with an initial launch weight of only <span class="nowrap">89.6 lbs (40.64 kg)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> (Earth\'s atmosphere at 80 to 100 miles (130 to 160&#160;km) altitude begins to have a significant drag effect on orbiting satellites and can be considered to end about that area.)\n</p><p>The Smithsonian was interested and asked Goddard to elaborate upon his initial inquiry. Goddard responded with a detailed manuscript he had already prepared, entitled <i>A Method of Reaching Extreme Altitudes</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-25"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>79</span></sup>\n</p><p>In January 1917, the Smithsonian agreed to provide Goddard with a five-year grant totaling <span class="nowrap">US$5000</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-26"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>84</span></sup> Afterward, Clark was able to contribute <span class="nowrap">US$3500 </span> and the use of their physics lab to the project. Worcester Polytechnic Institute also allowed him to use its abandoned Magnetics Laboratory on the edge of campus during this time, as a safe place for testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-27"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>85</span></sup>\n</p><p>It wasn\'t until two years later, at the insistence of Dr. Arthur G. Webster, the world-renowned head of Clark\'s physics department, that Goddard arranged for the Smithsonian to publish his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-28"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>102</span></sup>\n</p><p>While at Clark University, Goddard did research into solar power using a parabolic dish to concentrate the sun\'s rays on a machined piece of <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, that was sprayed with <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, which then heated water and drove an electric generator. Goddard believed his invention had overcome all the obstacles that had previously defeated other scientists and inventors, and he had his findings published in the November 1929 issue of  <i>Popular Science</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Goddard.27s_military_rocket"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Goddard\'s_military_rocket">Goddard\'s military rocket</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Goddard\'s military rocket">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Not all of Goddard\'s early work was geared towards space travel. As the United States entered World War I in 1917, the country\'s universities began to lend their services to the war effort. Goddard believed his rocket research could be applied to many different military applications, including mobile artillery, field weapons and naval <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedoes</a>. He made proposals to the Navy and Army. No record exists in his papers of any interest by the Navy to Goddard\'s inquiry. However, Army Ordnance was quite interested, and Goddard met several times with Army personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-29"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>89</span></sup>\n</p><p>During this time, Goddard was also contacted by a civilian industrialist in Worcester about the possibility of manufacturing rockets for the military. However, as the businessman\'s enthusiasm grew, so did Goddard\'s suspicion. Talks eventually broke down as Goddard began to fear his work might be appropriated by the business. However, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Army_Signal_Corps" title="Army Signal Corps">Army Signal Corps</a> officer tried to make Goddard cooperate, but he was called off by General George Squier of the Signal Corps who had been contacted by Secretary of the Smithsonian Institution, Charles Walcott.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-30"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>89&#8211;91</span></sup> Goddard became leery of working with corporations and was careful to secure patents to "protect his ideas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-31"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>152</span></sup> These events led to the Signal Corps sponsoring Goddard\'s work during World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-32"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>91</span></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard proposed to the Army an idea for a tube-based rocket launcher as a light infantry weapon. The launcher concept became the precursor to the <a href="/wiki/Bazooka" title="Bazooka">bazooka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-33"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>92</span></sup> The rocket-powered, recoil-free weapon was the brainchild of Goddard as a side project (under Army contract) of his work on rocket propulsion. Goddard, during his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenure" title="Tenure">tenure</a> at <a href="/wiki/Clark_University" title="Clark University">Clark University</a>, and working at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Mount Wilson Observatory</a> for security reasons, designed the tube-fired rocket for military use during World War I. He and his co-worker, Dr. <a href="/wiki/Clarence_N._Hickman" title="Clarence N. Hickman">Clarence N. Hickman</a> successfully demonstrated his rocket to the <a href="/wiki/Signal_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Signal Corps (United States Army)">U.S. Army Signal Corps</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen_Proving_Ground" title="Aberdeen Proving Ground">Aberdeen Proving Ground</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, on November 6, 1918, using two music stands for a launch platform. The Army was impressed, but the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armistice_with_Germany_(Compi%C3%A8gne)" title="Armistice with Germany (Compi&#232;gne)">Compi&#232;gne Armistice</a> was signed only five days later, and further development was discontinued as World War I ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_&#8211;_Dr._Robert_H._Goddard,_American_Rocketry_Pioneer_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_&#8211;_Dr._Robert_H._Goddard,_American_Rocketry_Pioneer-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The delay in the development of the bazooka and other weapons was a result of Goddard\'s serious bout with tuberculosis&#8212;the long recovery required. Goddard continued to be a part-time consultant to the U.S. Government at <a href="/wiki/Indian_Head,_Maryland" title="Indian Head, Maryland">Indian Head, Maryland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-34"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>121</span></sup> until 1923, but his focus had turned to other research involving rocket propulsion, including work with liquid fuels and liquid oxygen.\n</p><p>Later, the former Clark University researcher Dr. <a href="/wiki/Clarence_N._Hickman" title="Clarence N. Hickman">Clarence N. Hickman</a>, and Army officers Col. <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Skinner" title="Leslie Skinner">Leslie Skinner</a> and Lt. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Uhl" title="Edward Uhl">Edward Uhl</a> continued Goddard\'s work on the bazooka. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaped-charge" title="Shaped-charge">shaped-charge</a> warhead was attached to the rocket, leading to the tank-killing weapon used in World War II and to many other powerful rocket weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-35"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>305</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="A_Method_of_Reaching_Extreme_Altitudes"><i>A Method of Reaching Extreme Altitudes</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: A Method of Reaching Extreme Altitudes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 1919 Goddard thought that it would be premature to disclose the results of his experiments because his engine was not sufficiently developed. Dr. Webster realized that Goddard had accomplished a good deal of fine work and insisted that Goddard publish his progress so far or he would take care of it himself, so Goddard asked the Smithsonian Institution if it would publish the report he had submitted in late 1916.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-36"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>102</span></sup>\n</p><p>In late 1919, the Smithsonian published Goddard\'s groundbreaking work, <i>A Method of Reaching Extreme Altitudes</i>. The report describes Goddard\'s mathematical theories of rocket flight, his experiments with solid-fuel rockets, and the possibilities he saw of exploring Earth\'s atmosphere and beyond. Along with <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Tsiolkovsky" title="Konstantin Tsiolkovsky">Konstantin Tsiolkovsky</a>\'s earlier work, <i>The Exploration of Cosmic Space by Means of Reaction Devices</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsiolkovsky,_Exploration_of_Cosmic_Space_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsiolkovsky,_Exploration_of_Cosmic_Space-35">[35]</a></sup> which was not widely disseminated outside Russia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Goddard\'s book is regarded as one of the pioneering works of the science of rocketry, and 1750 copies were distributed worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emme_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emme-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard described extensive experiments with solid-fuel rocket engines burning high grade nitrocellulose <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_powder" title="Smokeless powder">smokeless powder</a>. A critical breakthrough was the use of the steam turbine nozzle invented by the Swedish inventor <a href="/wiki/Gustaf_de_Laval" title="Gustaf de Laval">Gustaf de Laval</a>. The <a href="/wiki/De_Laval_nozzle" title="De Laval nozzle">de Laval nozzle</a> allows the most efficient (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isentropic" title="Isentropic">isentropic</a>) conversion of the energy of hot gases into forward motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shapiro,_1953_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shapiro,_1953-38">[38]</a></sup> By means of this nozzle, Goddard increased the efficiency of his rocket engines from two percent to 64 percent and obtained supersonic exhaust velocities of over Mach 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>44</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goddard,_Rockets_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goddard,_Rockets-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though most of this work dealt with the theoretical and experimental relations between propellant, rocket mass, thrust, and velocity, a final section, entitled "Calculation of minimum mass required to raise one pound to an \'infinite\' altitude," discussed the possible uses of rockets, not only to reach the upper atmosphere but to <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape from Earth\'s gravitation altogether</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> He determined that a rocket with an effective exhaust velocity (see <a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">specific impulse</a>) of 7000 feet per second and an initial weight of 602 pounds would be able to send a one-pound payload to an infinite height. Included as a <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> was the idea of launching a rocket to the moon and igniting a mass of flash powder on its surface, so as to be visible through a telescope. He discussed the matter seriously, down to an estimate of the amount of powder required. Goddard\'s conclusion was that a rocket with starting mass of 3.21 tons could produce a flash "just visible" from Earth, assuming a final payload weight of 10.7 pounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard eschewed publicity, because he did not have time to reply to criticism of his work, and his imaginative ideas about space travel were shared only with private groups he trusted. He did, though, publish and talk about the rocket principle and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sounding_rockets" title="Sounding rockets">sounding rockets</a>, since these subjects were not too "far out." In a letter to the Smithsonian, dated March 1920, he discussed: photographing the Moon and planets from rocket-powered fly-by probes, sending messages to distant civilizations on inscribed metal plates, the use of solar energy in space, and the idea of high-velocity ion propulsion. In that same letter, Goddard clearly describes the concept of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_reentry#Ablative" title="Atmospheric reentry">ablative heat shield</a>, suggesting the landing apparatus be covered with "layers of a very infusible hard substance with layers of a poor heat conductor between" designed to erode in the same way as the surface of a meteor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:33%; ; background-color: #F5F5F5;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8220; ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8221; ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">Every vision is a joke until the first man accomplishes it; once realized, it becomes commonplace.</div>\n<p><cite class="center-aligned" style="">&#8211;Response to a reporter\'s question following criticism in <i>The New York Times</i>, 1920.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_University_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_University-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-43">[43]</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Publicity_and_criticism">Publicity and criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Publicity and criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The publication of Goddard\'s document gained him national attention from U.S. newspapers, most of it negative. Although Goddard\'s discussion of targeting the moon was only a small part of the work as a whole (eight lines on the next to last page of 69 pages), and was intended as an illustration of the possibilities rather than a declaration of intent, the papers sensationalized his ideas to the point of misrepresentation and ridicule. Even the Smithsonian had to abstain from publicity because of the amount of ridiculous correspondence received from the general public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>113</span></sup> David Lasser, who co-founded the <a href="/wiki/American_Rocket_Society" title="American Rocket Society">American Rocket Society</a>, wrote in 1931 that Goddard was subjected in the press to the "most violent attacks."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lasser_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lasser-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On January 12, 1920, a front-page story in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, "Believes Rocket Can Reach Moon", reported a Smithsonian press release about a "multiple-charge, high-efficiency rocket." The chief application envisaged was "the possibility of sending recording apparatus to moderate and extreme altitudes within the Earth\'s atmosphere", the advantage over balloon-carried instruments being ease of recovery, since "the new rocket apparatus would go straight up and come straight down." But it also mentioned a proposal "to [send] to the dark part of the new moon a sufficiently large amount of the most brilliant flash powder which, in being ignited on impact, would be plainly visible in a powerful telescope. This would be the only way of proving that the rocket had really left the attraction of the earth, as the apparatus would never come back, once it had escaped that attraction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_Times_editorial"><i>New York Times</i> editorial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: New York Times editorial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>On January 13, 1920, the day after its front-page story about Goddard\'s rocket, an unsigned <i>New York Times</i> editorial, in a section entitled "Topics of the Times", scoffed at the proposal. The article, which bore the title "A Severe Strain on Credulity",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> began with apparent approval, but soon went on to cast serious doubt:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>As a method of sending a missile to the higher, and even highest, part of the earth\'s atmospheric envelope, Professor Goddard\'s multiple-charge rocket is a practicable, and therefore promising device. Such a rocket, too, might carry self-recording instruments, to be released at the limit of its flight, and conceivable parachutes would bring them safely to the ground. It is not obvious, however, that the instruments would return to the point of departure; indeed, it is obvious that they would not, for parachutes drift exactly as balloons do. And the rocket, or what was left of it after the last explosion, would need to be aimed with amazing skill, and in a dead calm, to fall on the spot whence it started. [New paragraph.] But that is a slight inconvenience, at least from the scientific standpoint, though it might be serious enough from that of the always innocent bystander a few hundred or thousand yards from the firing line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT12_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT12-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The article pressed further on Goddard\'s proposal to launch rockets beyond the atmosphere:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>[A]fter the rocket quits our air and really starts on its longer journey, its flight would be neither accelerated nor maintained by the explosion of the charges it then might have left. To claim that it would be is to deny a fundamental law of dynamics, and only Dr. Einstein and his chosen dozen, so few and fit, are licensed to do that.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The basis of that criticism was the then-common belief that thrust was produced by the rocket exhaust pushing against the atmosphere; Goddard realized that Newton\'s third law (reaction) was the actual principle.\n</p><p>Finally, in the follow-on section, "His plan is not original", the writer assumed, wrongly, that Goddard\'s understanding of Newton\'s laws was flawed:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>That Professor Goddard, with his "chair" in Clark College and the countenancing of the Smithsonian Institution, does not know the relation of action and reaction, and of the need to have something better than a vacuum against which to react&#8212;to say that would be absurd. Of course he only seems to lack the knowledge ladled out daily in high schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT12_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT12-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Unbeknownst to the <i>Times</i>, thrust is possible in a vacuum, as the writer would have discovered had he read Goddard\'s paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A week after the <i>New York Times</i> editorial, Goddard released a signed statement to the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a>, attempting to restore reason to what had become a sensational story:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Too much attention has been concentrated on the proposed flash pow[d]er experiment, and too little on the exploration of the atmosphere. ... Whatever interesting possibilities there may be of the method that has been proposed, other than the purpose for which it was intended, no one of them could be undertaken without first exploring the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In 1924, Goddard published an article, "How my speed rocket can propel itself in vacuum", in <i><a href="/wiki/Popular_Science" title="Popular Science">Popular Science</a></i>, in which he explained the physics and gave details of the vacuum experiments he had performed to prove the theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> But, no matter how he tried to explain his results, he was not understood. After one of Goddard\'s experiments in 1929, a local Worcester newspaper carried the mocking headline "Moon rocket misses target by 238,799&#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>&#160;miles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard worked alone with just his team of mechanics and machinists for many years.  This was a result of the harsh criticism from the media and other scientists, and his understanding of the military applications which foreign powers might use. Goddard became increasingly suspicious of others and often worked alone, except during the two World Wars, which limited the impact of much of his work. Another limiting factor was the lack of support from the American government, military and academia, all failing to understand the value of the rocket to study the atmosphere and near space, and for military applications. As Germany became ever more war-like, he refused to communicate with German rocket experimenters, though he received more and more of their correspondence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-37"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>131&#8211;3,166</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id=".27A_Correction.27"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="\'A_Correction\'">\'A Correction\'</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: \'A Correction\'">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Forty-nine years after its editorial mocking Goddard, on July 17, 1969&#8212;the day after the launch of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a>&#8212;<i>The New York Times</i> published a short item under the headline "A Correction." The three-paragraph statement summarized its 1920 editorial, and concluded:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Further investigation and experimentation have confirmed the findings of Isaac Newton in the 17th Century and it is now definitely established that a rocket can function in a vacuum as well as in an atmosphere. The Times regrets the error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_liquid-fueled_flight">First liquid-fueled flight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: First liquid-fueled flight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_static_tests">First static tests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: First static tests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2946" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg/170px-Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg/255px-Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg/340px-Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Robert Goddard, bundled against the cold weather of March 16, 1926, holds the launching frame of his most notable invention &#8212; the first liquid-fueled rocket.</div></div></div>\n<p>Goddard began experimenting with liquid oxidizer, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_fuel_rocket" title="Liquid fuel rocket">liquid fuel rockets</a> in September 1921, and successfully tested the first liquid propellant engine in November 1923.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>520</span></sup> It had a cylindrical <a href="/wiki/Combustion_chamber" title="Combustion chamber">combustion chamber</a>, using impinging jets to mix and atomize <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>499&#8211;500</span></sup>\n</p><p>In 1924&#8211;25, Goddard had problems developing a high-pressure <a href="/wiki/Piston_pump" title="Piston pump">piston pump</a> to send fuel to the combustion chamber. He wanted to scale up the experiments, but his funding would not allow such growth. He decided to forego the pumps and use a pressurized fuel feed system applying pressure to the fuel tank from a tank of <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a>, a technique used today. The liquid oxygen, some of which evaporated,  provided its own pressure.\n</p><p>On December 6, 1925, he tested the simpler pressure feed system. He conducted a static test on the firing stand at the Clark University physics laboratory. The engine successfully lifted its own weight in a 27-second test in the static rack. It was a major success for Goddard, proving that a liquid fuel rocket was possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-38"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>140</span></sup> The test moved Goddard an important step closer to launching a rocket with liquid fuel.\n</p><p>Goddard conducted an additional test in December, and two more in January 1926. After that, he began preparing for a possible launch of the rocket system.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_flight">First flight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: First flight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Goddard launched the first liquid-fueled (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipropellant_rocket" title="Bipropellant rocket">gasoline and liquid oxygen</a>) rocket on March 16, 1926, in <a href="/wiki/Auburn,_Massachusetts" title="Auburn, Massachusetts">Auburn, Massachusetts</a>. Present at the launch were his crew chief, Henry Sachs, Esther Goddard, and Percy Roope, who was Clark\'s assistant professor in the physics department. Goddard\'s diary entry of the event was notable for its understatement:\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>March 16. Went to Auburn with S[achs] in am. E[sther] and Mr. Roope came out at 1 p.m. Tried rocket at 2.30. It rose 41 feet &amp; went 184 feet, in 2.5 secs., after the lower half of the nozzle burned off. Brought materials to lab. ...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-39"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>143</span></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>His diary entry the next day elaborated:\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>March 17, 1926. The first flight with a rocket using liquid propellants was made yesterday at Aunt Effie\'s farm in Auburn. ... \nEven though the release was pulled, the rocket did not rise at first, but the flame came out, and there was a steady roar. After a number of seconds it rose, slowly until it cleared the frame, and then at express train speed, curving over to the left, and striking the ice and snow, still going at a rapid rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-40"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>143</span></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The rocket, which was later dubbed "Nell", rose just 41&#160;feet during a 2.5-second flight that ended 184 feet away in a cabbage field,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> but it was an important demonstration that liquid fuels and oxidizers were possible propellants for larger rockets. The launch site is now a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Rocket_Launching_Site" title="Goddard Rocket Launching Site">Goddard Rocket Launching Site</a>.\n</p><p>Viewers familiar with more modern rocket designs may find it difficult to distinguish the rocket from its launching apparatus in the well-known picture of "Nell". The complete rocket is significantly taller than Goddard, but does not include the pyramidal support structure which he is grasping. The rocket\'s <a href="/wiki/Combustion_chamber" title="Combustion chamber">combustion chamber</a> is the small cylinder at the top; the <a href="/wiki/Nozzle" title="Nozzle">nozzle</a> is visible beneath it. The fuel tank, which is also part of the rocket, is the larger cylinder opposite Goddard\'s torso. The fuel tank is directly beneath the nozzle, and is protected from the motor\'s exhaust by an <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> cone. Asbestos-wrapped aluminum tubes connect the motor to the tanks, providing both support and fuel transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> This layout is no longer used, since the experiment showed that this was <a href="/wiki/Pendulum_rocket_fallacy" title="Pendulum rocket fallacy">no more stable</a> than placing the combustion chamber and nozzle at the base. By May, after a series of modifications to simplify the plumbing, the combustion chamber and nozzle were placed in the now classic position, at the lower end of the rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sutton_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>259</span></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard determined early that fins alone were not sufficient to stabilize the rocket in flight and keep it on the desired trajectory in the face of winds aloft and other  disturbing forces. He added movable vanes in the exhaust, controlled by a gyroscope, to control and steer his rocket. (The Germans used this technique in their V-2.) He also introduced the more efficient swiveling engine in several rockets, basically the method used to steer large liquid-propellant missiles and launchers today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sutton_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>263&#8211;6</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lindbergh_and_Goddard">Lindbergh and Goddard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Lindbergh and Goddard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>After a launch of one of Goddard\'s rockets in July 1929 again gained the attention of the newspapers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a> learned of his work in a <i>New York Times</i> article. At the time, Lindbergh had begun to wonder what would become of <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a> (even space flight) in the distant future and had settled on jet propulsion and rocket flight as a probable next step. After checking with the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> (MIT) and being assured that Goddard was a bona fide physicist and not a crackpot, he phoned Goddard in November 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>141</span></sup> Professor Goddard met the aviator soon after, in his office at Clark University.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lehm_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-lehm-57">[57]</a></sup> Upon meeting Goddard, Lindbergh was immediately impressed by his research, and Goddard was similarly impressed by the flier\'s interest. He discussed his work openly with Lindbergh, forming an alliance that would last for the rest of his life. While having long since become reticent to share his ideas, Goddard showed complete openness with those few who shared his dream, and whom he felt he could trust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lehm_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-lehm-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By late 1929, Goddard had been attracting additional notoriety with each rocket launch. He was finding it increasingly difficult to conduct his research without unwanted distractions. Lindbergh discussed finding additional financing for Goddard\'s work, and lent his famous name to Goddard\'s work.
