In 1930 Lindbergh made several proposals to industry and private investors for funding, which proved all but impossible to find following the recent <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_of_1929" title="Wall Street Crash of 1929">U.S. stock market crash</a> in October 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lehm_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-lehm-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guggenheim_sponsorship">Guggenheim sponsorship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Guggenheim sponsorship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the spring of 1930, Lindbergh finally found an ally in the <a href="/wiki/Guggenheim_family" title="Guggenheim family">Guggenheim family</a>. Financier <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Guggenheim" title="Daniel Guggenheim">Daniel Guggenheim</a> agreed to fund Goddard\'s research over the next four years for a total of $100,000 (~$1.8&#160;million today). The Guggenheim family, especially <a href="/wiki/Harry_Frank_Guggenheim" title="Harry Frank Guggenheim">Harry Guggenheim</a>, would continue to support Goddard\'s work in the years to come. The Goddards soon moved to <a href="/wiki/Roswell,_New_Mexico" title="Roswell, New Mexico">Roswell, New Mexico</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lehm_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-lehm-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because of the military potential of the rocket, Goddard, Lindbergh, Harry Guggenheim, the Smithsonian Institution and others tried in 1940, before the U.S. entered World War II, to convince the Army and Navy of its value. Goddard\'s services were offered, but there was no interest, initially. Two young, imaginative officers eventually got the services to attempt to contract with Goddard just prior to the war. The Navy beat the Army to the punch and secured his services to build liquid-fueled rockets for jet-assisted take-off (JATO) of aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-41"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>293&#8211;297</span></sup> These rockets were the precursors to some of the large rocket engines that launched the space age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glenn_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lack_of_vision_in_the_United_States">Lack of vision in the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Lack of vision in the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Before World War II there was a lack of vision and serious interest in the United States concerning the potential of rocketry, especially in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington</a>. Although the Weather Bureau was interested beginning in 1929 in Goddard\'s rocket for atmospheric research, the Bureau could not secure governmental funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>719,746</span></sup> Between the World Wars, the <a href="/wiki/Daniel_and_Florence_Guggenheim_Foundation" title="Daniel and Florence Guggenheim Foundation">Guggenheim Foundation</a> was the main source of funding for Goddard\'s research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braun-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>46,59,60</span></sup> Goddard\'s liquid-fueled rocket was neglected by his country, according to aerospace historian Eugene Emme, but was noticed and advanced by other nations, especially the Germans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emme_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emme-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>63</span></sup> Goddard showed remarkable prescience in 1923 in a letter to the Smithsonian. He knew that the Germans were very interested in rocketry and said he "would not be surprised if the research would become something in the nature of a race" and he wondered how soon the European "theorists" would begin to build rockets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-42"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>136</span></sup>\nIn 1936, the U.S. military attach&#233; in Berlin asked Charles Lindbergh to visit Germany and learn what he could of their progress in aviation. Although the Luftwaffe showed him their factories and were open concerning their growing airpower, they were silent on the subject of rocketry. When Lindbergh told Goddard of this behavior, Goddard said, "Yes, they must have plans for the rocket. When will our own people in Washington listen to reason?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-43"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>272</span></sup>\n</p><p>Most of the U.S.\'s largest universities were also slow to realize rocketry\'s potential. Just before World War II, the head of the aeronautics department at MIT, at a meeting held by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Corps" title="United States Army Air Corps">Army Air Corps</a> to discuss project funding, said that the <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a> (Caltech) "can take the Buck Rogers Job [rocket research]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carter_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-60">[60]</a></sup> In 1941, Goddard tried to recruit an engineer for his team from MIT but couldn\'t find one who was interested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-44"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>326</span></sup>  There were some exceptions: MIT was at least teaching basic rocketry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-45"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>264</span></sup> and Caltech had courses in rocketry and aerodynamics. After the war, Dr. Jerome Hunsaker of MIT, having studied Goddard\'s patents, stated that "Every liquid-fuel rocket that flies is a Goddard rocket."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-46"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>363</span></sup>\n</p><p>While away in Roswell, Goddard was still head of the physics department at Clark University, and Clark deserves credit for allowing him to devote most of his time to rocket research. Likewise the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Los_Angeles" title="University of California, Los Angeles">University of California, Los Angeles</a> (UCLA) permitted astronomer Samuel Herrick to pursue research in space vehicle guidance and control, and shortly after the war to teach courses in spacecraft guidance and orbit determination. Herrick began corresponding with Goddard in 1931 and asked if he should work in this new field, which he named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrodynamics" title="Astrodynamics">astrodynamics</a>. Herrick said that Goddard had the vision to advise and encourage him in his use of <a href="/wiki/Celestial_mechanics" title="Celestial mechanics">celestial mechanics</a> "to anticipate the basic problem of space navigation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Roswell.2C_New_Mexico"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Roswell,_New_Mexico">Roswell, New Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Roswell, New Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RobertHGoddardRocket_TopDown.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2815" data-file-width="2199" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/RobertHGoddardRocket_TopDown.jpg/170px-RobertHGoddardRocket_TopDown.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/RobertHGoddardRocket_TopDown.jpg/255px-RobertHGoddardRocket_TopDown.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/RobertHGoddardRocket_TopDown.jpg/340px-RobertHGoddardRocket_TopDown.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RobertHGoddardRocket_TopDown.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a> took this picture of Robert H. Goddard\'s rocket, when he peered down the launching tower on September 23, 1935, in Roswell, New Mexico.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Goddard_Transports_Rocket_-_GPN-2000-001693.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2293" data-file-width="2952" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Dr._Goddard_Transports_Rocket_-_GPN-2000-001693.jpg/220px-Dr._Goddard_Transports_Rocket_-_GPN-2000-001693.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Dr._Goddard_Transports_Rocket_-_GPN-2000-001693.jpg/330px-Dr._Goddard_Transports_Rocket_-_GPN-2000-001693.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Dr._Goddard_Transports_Rocket_-_GPN-2000-001693.jpg/440px-Dr._Goddard_Transports_Rocket_-_GPN-2000-001693.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Goddard_Transports_Rocket_-_GPN-2000-001693.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Goddard towing a rocket in Roswell</div></div></div>\n<p>With new financial backing, Goddard eventually relocated to Roswell, New Mexico, in summer of 1930,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gainor_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gainor-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>46</span></sup> where he worked with his team of technicians in near-isolation and relative secrecy for years. He had consulted a meteorologist as to the best area to do his work, and Roswell seemed ideal. Here they would not endanger anyone, would not be bothered by the curious, and would experience a more moderate climate (which was also better for Goddard\'s health).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-47"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>177</span></sup> The locals valued personal privacy, knew Goddard desired his, and when travelers asked where Goddard\'s facilities were located, they would likely be misdirected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-48"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>261</span></sup>\n</p><p>By September 1931, his rockets had the now familiar appearance of a smooth casing with tail-fins. He began experimenting with <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopic</a> guidance, and made a flight test of such a system in April 1932. A gyroscope mounted on gimbals electrically controlled steering vanes in the exhaust, similar to the system used by the German <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2</a> over 10&#160;years later. Though the rocket crashed after a short ascent, the guidance system had worked, and Goddard considered the test a success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-49"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>193&#8211;5</span></sup>\n</p><p>A temporary loss of funding from the Guggenheims, as a result of the depression, forced Goddard in spring of 1932 to return to Clark University until the autumn of 1934, when funding resumed. Upon his return to Roswell, he began work on his A series of rockets, 4 to 4.5&#160;meters long, and powered by gasoline and liquid oxygen pressurized with nitrogen. The gyroscopic control system was housed in the middle of the rocket, between the propellant tanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RockDev_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-RockDev-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>xv,15&#8211;46</span></sup>\n</p><p>The A-4 used a simpler pendulum system for guidance, as the gyroscopic system was being repaired. On March 8, 1935 it flew up to 1,000 feet, then turned into the wind and, Goddard reported, "roared in a powerful descent across the prairie, at close to, or at, the speed of sound." On March 28, 1935, the A-5 successfully flew vertically to an altitude of (0.91&#160;mi; 4,800&#160;ft) using his gyroscopic guidance system. It then turned to a nearly horizontal path, flew 13,000 feet and achieved a maximum speed of 550 miles per hour. Goddard was elated because the guidance system kept the rocket on a vertical path so well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-50"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>208</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>978&#8211;9</span></sup>\n</p><p>In 1936&#8211;1939, Goddard began work on the K and L series rockets, which were much more massive and designed to reach very high altitude. The K series consisted of static bench tests of a more powerful engine, achieving a thrust of 624&#160;lbs.in February 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Braun-59">[59]</a></sup>  This work was plagued by trouble with chamber burn-through. In 1923, Goddard had built a regeneratively cooled engine, which circulated liquid oxygen around the outside of the combustion chamber, but he deemed the idea too complicated. He then used a curtain cooling method that involved spraying excess gasoline, which evaporated around the inside wall of the combustion chamber, but this scheme did not work well, and the larger rockets failed. Returning to a smaller design, the L-13 reached an altitude of 2.7 kilometers (1.7&#160;mi; 8,900&#160;ft), the highest of any of Goddard\'s rockets. Weight was reduced by using thin-walled fuel tanks wound with high-tensile-strength wire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RockDev_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-RockDev-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>71&#8211;148</span></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard experimented with many of the features of today\'s large rockets, such as multiple combustion chambers and nozzles. In November, 1936, he flew the world\'s first rocket (L-7) with multiple chambers, hoping to increase thrust without increasing the size of a single chamber. It had four combustion chambers, reached a height of 200 feet, and corrected its vertical path using blast vanes until one chamber burned through. This flight demonstrated that a rocket with multiple combustion chambers could fly stably and be easily guided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RockDev_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-RockDev-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>96</span></sup>\n</p><p>From 1940 to 1941, work was done on the P series of rockets, which used propellant turbopumps (also powered by gasoline and liquid oxygen). The lightweight pumps produced higher propellant pressures, permitting a more powerful engine (greater thrust) and a lighter structure (lighter tanks and no pressurization tank), but two launches both ended in crashes after reaching an altitude of only a few hundred feet. The turbopumps worked well, however, and Goddard was pleased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RockDev_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-RockDev-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>187&#8211;215</span></sup>\n</p><p>When Goddard mentioned the need for turbopumps, Harry Guggenheim suggested that he contact pump manufacturers to aid him. None were interested, as the development cost of these miniature pumps was prohibitive. Goddard\'s team was therefore left on its own and from September 1938 to June 1940 designed and tested the small turbopumps and gas generators to operate the turbines. Esther later said that the pump tests were "the most trying and disheartening phase of the research."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-51"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>274&#8211;5</span></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard was able to flight-test many of his rockets, but many resulted in what the uninitiated would call failures, usually resulting from engine malfunction or loss of control. Goddard did not consider them failures, however, because he felt that he always learned something from a test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gainor_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gainor-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>45</span></sup> Most of his work involved static tests, which are a standard procedure today, before a flight test.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_Jimmy_Doolittle">General Jimmy Doolittle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: General Jimmy Doolittle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Doolittle" title="Jimmy Doolittle">Jimmy Doolittle</a> was introduced to the field of space science at an early point in its history. He recalls in his autobiography, "I became interested in rocket development in the 1930s when I met Robert H. Goddard, who laid the foundation. ... While with Shell Oil I worked with him on the development of a type of fuel. ... "<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jimmy_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jimmy-63">[63]</a></sup> Harry Guggenheim and Charles Lindbergh arranged for (then Major) Doolittle to discuss with Goddard a special blend of gasoline. Doolittle flew himself to Roswell in October 1938 and was given a tour of Goddard\'s shop and a "short course" in rocketry.  He then wrote a memo, including a rather detailed description of Goddard\'s rocket. In closing he said, "interplanetary transportation is probably a dream of the very distant future, but with the moon only a quarter of a million miles away&#8212;who knows!" In July 1941 he wrote Goddard that he  was still interested in his rocket propulsion research. The Army was interested only in JATO at this point. However, Doolittle and Lindbergh were concerned about the state of rocketry in the US, and Doolittle remained in touch with Goddard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1208&#8211;16,1334,1443</span></sup>\n</p><p>Shortly after World War II Doolittle spoke to an American Rocket Society conference at which a  large number interested in rocketry attended. His talk concerned Dr. Robert Goddard. He later stated that at that time "we [in the aeronautics field] had not given much credence to the tremendous potential of rocketry."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Putnam_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Putnam-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1956 he was appointed chairman of the <a href="/wiki/National_Advisory_Committee_for_Aeronautics" title="National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics">National Adviser Committee for Aeronautics</a> (NACA) because the previous chairman, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jerome_C._Hunsaker" title="Jerome C. Hunsaker">Jerome C. Hunsaker</a>, thought Doolittle to be more sympathetic than other scientists and engineers to the rocket, which was increasing in importance as a scientific tool as well as a weapon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jimmy_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jimmy-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>516</span></sup> Doolittle was instrumental in the successful transition of the NACA to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> (NASA) in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heppenheimer_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heppenheimer-65">[65]</a></sup> He was offered the position as first administrator of NASA, but he turned it down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Putnam_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Putnam-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_history">Launch history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Launch history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Between 1926 and 1941, the following 35 rockets were launched:<sup class="reference" class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goddard_rocket_parts.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3443" data-file-width="1930" decoding="async" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Goddard_rocket_parts.JPG/170px-Goddard_rocket_parts.JPG" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Goddard_rocket_parts.JPG/340px-Goddard_rocket_parts.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goddard_rocket_parts.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some of the parts of Goddard\'s rockets</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_of_results">Analysis of results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Analysis of results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As an instrument for reaching extreme altitudes, Goddard\'s rockets were not very successful; they did not achieve an altitude greater than 2.7&#160;km in 1937, while a balloon sonde had already reached 35&#160;km in 1921.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>456</span></sup> By contrast, German rocket scientists had achieved an altitude of 2.4&#160;km with the A-2 rocket in 1934,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gruntman_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gruntman-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>138</span></sup> 8&#160;km by 1939 with the A-5,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ordway_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ordway-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>39</span></sup> and 196&#160;km in 1942 with the A-4 (<a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2</a>) launched vertically, reaching the outer limits of the atmosphere and into space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>221</span></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard\'s pace was slower than the Germans\' because he did not have the resources they did. Simply reaching high altitudes was not his primary goal; he was trying, with a methodical approach, to perfect his liquid fuel engine and subsystems such as guidance and control so that his rocket could eventually achieve high altitudes without tumbling in the rare atmosphere, providing a stable vehicle for the experiments it would eventually carry. He had built the necessary turbopumps and was on the verge of building larger, more reliable rockets to reach extreme altitudes when World War II intervened and changed the path of American history. He hoped to return to his experiments in Roswell after the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-52"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>206,230,330&#8211;1</span></sup>\n</p><p>Although Goddard had brought his work in rocketry to the attention of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a>, between World Wars, he was rebuffed, since the Army largely failed to grasp the military application of large rockets and said there was no money for new experimental weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-53"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>297</span></sup> German military intelligence, by contrast, had paid attention to Goddard\'s work. The Goddards noticed that some mail had been opened, and some mailed reports had gone missing. An accredited <a href="/wiki/Military_attach%C3%A9" title="Military attach&#233;">military attach&#233;</a> to the US, Friedrich von Boetticher, sent a four-page report to the <i><a href="/wiki/Abwehr" title="Abwehr">Abwehr</a></i> in 1936, and the spy Gustav Guellich sent a mixture of facts and made-up information, claiming to have visited Roswell and witnessed a launch. The <i>Abwehr</i> was very interested and responded with more questions about Goddard\'s work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>77</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>227&#8211;8</span></sup> Guellich\'s reports did include information about fuel mixtures and the important concept of fuel-curtain cooling,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>39&#8211;41</span></sup> but thereafter the Germans received very little information about Goddard.\n</p><p>The Soviet Union had a spy in the U.S. Navy Bureau of Aeronautics. In 1935, she gave them a report Goddard had written for the Navy in 1933. It contained results of tests and flights and suggestions for military uses of his rockets. The Soviets considered this to be very valuable information. It provided few design details, but gave them the direction and knowledge about Goddard\'s progress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haynes,_Spies,_2009_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haynes,_Spies,_2009-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>386&#8211;7</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Annapolis.2C_Maryland"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Annapolis,_Maryland">Annapolis, Maryland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Annapolis, Maryland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Navy Lieutenant Charles F. Fischer, who had visited Goddard in Roswell earlier and gained his confidence, believed Goddard was doing valuable work and was able to convince the Bureau of Aeronautics in September 1941 that Goddard could build the JATO unit the Navy desired. While still in Roswell, and before the Navy contract took effect, Goddard began in September to apply his technology to build a variable-thrust engine to be attached to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PBY" title="PBY">PBY</a> seaplane. By May 1942 he had a unit that could meet the Navy\'s requirements and be able to launch a heavily loaded aircraft from a short runway. In February he received part of a PBY with bullet holes apparently acquired in the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a> attack. Goddard wrote to Guggenheim that "I can think of nothing that would give me greater satisfaction than to have it contribute to the inevitable retaliation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-54"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>322,328&#8211;9,331,335,337</span></sup>\n</p><p>In April Fischer notified Goddard that the Navy wanted to do all its rocket work at the Engineering Experiment Station at Annapolis. Esther, worried that a move to the climate of Maryland would cause Robert\'s health to deteriorate faster, objected. But the patriotic Goddard replied, "Esther, don\'t you know there\'s a war on?" Fischer also questioned the move, as Goddard could work just as well in Roswell. Goddard simply answered, "I was wondering when you would ask me." Fischer had wanted to offer him something bigger&#8212;a long range missile&#8212;but JATO was all he could manage, hoping for a greater project later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-55"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>338,9</span></sup> It was a case of a square peg in a round hole, according to a disappointed Goddard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-8"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>209</span></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard and his team had already been in Annapolis a month and had tested his constant-thrust JATO engine when he received a Navy telegram, forwarded from Roswell, ordering him to Annapolis. Lt. Fischer asked for a crash effort. By August his engine was producing 800&#160;lbs of thrust for 20 seconds, and Fischer was anxious to try it on a PBY. On the sixth test run, with all bugs worked out, the PBY, piloted by Fischer, was pushed into the air from the Severn River. Fischer landed and prepared to launch again. Goddard had wanted to check the unit, but radio contact with the PBY had been lost. On the seventh try the engine caught fire. The plane was 150 feet up when flight was aborted. Because Goddard had installed a safety feature at the last minute there was no explosion and no lives were lost. The problem\'s cause was traced to hasty installation and rough handling. Cheaper, safer solid fuel JATO engines were eventually selected by the armed forces. An engineer later said, "Putting [Goddard\'s] rocket on a seaplane was like hitching an eagle to a plow."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-56"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>344&#8211;50</span></sup>\n</p><p>Despite Goddard\'s efforts to convince the Navy that liquid-fueled rockets had greater potential, he said that the Navy had no interest in long-range missiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1554</span></sup> However, the Navy asked him to perfect the throttleable JATO engine. Goddard made improvements to the engine, and in November it was demonstrated to the Navy and some officials from Washington. Fischer invited the spectators to operate the controls; the engine blasted out over the Severn at full throttle with no hesitation, idled, and roared again at various thrust levels. The test was perfect, exceeding the Navy\'s requirements. The unit was able to be stopped and restarted, and it produced a medium thrust of 600 pounds for 15 seconds and a full thrust of 1,000 pounds for over 15 seconds. A Navy Commander commented that "It was like being Thor, playing with thunderbolts." Goddard had produced the essential propulsion control system of the rocket plane. The Goddards celebrated by attending the Army-Navy football game and attending the Fischers\' cocktail party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-9"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>350&#8211;1</span></sup> This engine was the basis of the Curtiss-Wright XLR25-CW-1 two-chamber, 15,000-pound thrust engine that powered the <a href="/wiki/Bell_X-2" title="Bell X-2">Bell X-2</a> research rocket plane. After World War II Goddard\'s team and some patents went to <a href="/wiki/Curtiss-Wright" title="Curtiss-Wright">Curtiss-Wright</a> Corporation. "Although his death in August 1945 prevented him from participating in the actual development of this engine, it was a direct descendent of his design."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Method_27-10"><a href="#cite_note-Method-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1606</span></sup> In September 1956 the X-2 was the first plane to reach 126,000 feet altitude and in its last flight exceeded Mach 3 (3.2) before losing control and crashing. The X-2 program advanced technology in areas such as steel alloys and aerodynamics at high Mach numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="V-2">V-2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: V-2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:33%; ; background-color: #F5F5F5;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">Don\'t you know about your own rocket pioneer? Dr. Goddard was ahead of us all.</div>\n<p><cite class="center-aligned" style="">&#8211;<a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a>, when asked about his work, following World War II<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>In the spring of 1945, Goddard saw a captured German V-2 ballistic missile, in the naval laboratory in Annapolis, Maryland, where he had been working under contract. The unlaunched rocket had been captured by the US Army from the <a href="/wiki/Mittelwerk" title="Mittelwerk">Mittelwerk</a> factory in the <a href="/wiki/Harz" title="Harz">Harz</a> mountains, and samples began to be shipped by <a href="/wiki/Operation_Paperclip#Related_operations" title="Operation Paperclip">Special Mission V-2</a> on 22 May 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ordway_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ordway-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a thorough inspection, Goddard was convinced that the Germans had "stolen" his work. Though the design details were not exactly the same, the basic design of the V-2 was similar to one of Goddard\'s rockets. The V-2, however, was technically far more advanced than the most successful of the rockets designed and tested by Goddard. The <a href="/wiki/Peenem%C3%BCnde_Army_Research_Center" title="Peenem&#252;nde Army Research Center">Peenem&#252;nde rocket group</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a> may have benefited from the pre-1939 contacts to a limited extent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-57"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>387&#8211;8</span></sup> but had also started from the work of their own space pioneer, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Oberth" title="Hermann Oberth">Hermann Oberth</a>; they also had the benefit of intensive state funding, large-scale production facilities (using slave labor), and repeated flight-testing that allowed them to refine their designs. Oberth was a theorist and had never built a rocket or a working engine.\n</p><p>Nevertheless, in 1963, von Braun, reflecting on the history of rocketry, said of Goddard: "His rockets ... may have been rather crude by present-day standards, but they blazed the trail and incorporated many features used in our most modern rockets and space vehicles".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> He once recalled that "Goddard\'s experiments in liquid fuel saved us years of work, and enabled us to perfect the V-2 years before it would have been possible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levine_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levine-74">[74]</a></sup> After  World War II von Braun reviewed Goddard\'s patents and believed they contained enough technical information to build a large missile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Three features developed by Goddard appeared in the V-2: (1) turbopumps were used to inject fuel into the combustion chamber; (2) gyroscopically controlled vanes in the nozzle stabilized the rocket until external vanes in the air could do so; and (3) excess alcohol was fed in around the combustion chamber walls, so that a blanket of evaporating gas protected the engine walls from the combustion heat.\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Germans had been watching Goddard\'s progress before the war and became convinced that large, liquid fuel rockets were feasible. General <a href="/wiki/Walter_Dornberger" title="Walter Dornberger">Walter Dornberger</a>, head of the V-2 project, used the idea that they were in a race with the U.S. and that Goddard had "disappeared" (to work with the Navy) to persuade Hitler to raise the priority of the V-2. It was a strategic mistake, however, to expend an estimated one-half billion war-era-dollars (not counting slave labor) for a terror weapon that did not create the fear desired and lacked the accuracy to be very effective against military targets. Resources could have been better used on existing, or new more effective, weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Goddard.27s_secrecy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Goddard\'s_secrecy">Goddard\'s secrecy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Goddard\'s secrecy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Goddard avoided sharing details of his work with other scientists, and preferred to work alone with his technicians. <a href="/wiki/Frank_Malina" title="Frank Malina">Frank Malina</a>, who was then studying rocketry at the <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a>, visited Goddard in August 1936. Goddard hesitated to discuss any of his research, other than that which had already been published in <i>Liquid-Propellant Rocket Development</i>. <a href="/wiki/Theodore_von_K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n" title="Theodore von K&#225;rm&#225;n">Theodore von K&#225;rm&#225;n</a>, Malina\'s mentor at the time, was unhappy with Goddard\'s attitude and later wrote, "Naturally we at Caltech wanted as much information as we could get from Goddard for our mutual benefit. But Goddard believed in secrecy. ... The trouble with secrecy is that one can easily go in the wrong direction and never know it." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burrows_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burrows-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>90</span></sup> However, at an earlier point von K&#225;rm&#225;n said that Malina was "highly enthusiastic" after his visit and that Caltech made changes to their liquid-propellant rocket, based on Goddard\'s work and patents. Malina remembered his visit as friendly and that he saw all but a few components in Goddard\'s shop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clary_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-Clary-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>178</span></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard\'s concerns about secrecy led to criticism for failure to cooperate with other scientists and engineers. His approach at that time was that independent development of his ideas without interference would bring quicker results even though he received less technical support. George Sutton, who became a rocket scientist working with von Braun\'s team in the late 1940s, said that he and his fellow workers had not heard of Goddard or his contributions, and that they would have saved time if they had known the details of his work. Sutton admits that it may have been their fault for not looking for Goddard\'s patents and depending on the German team for knowledge and guidance; he wrote that information about the patents was not well distributed in the U.S. at that early period, though Germany and the Soviet Union had copies of some of them. (The Patent Office did not release rocket patents during World War II.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sutton_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton-55">[55]</a></sup> However, the Aerojet Engineering Corporation, an offshoot of the <a href="/wiki/Guggenheim_Aeronautical_Laboratory" title="Guggenheim Aeronautical Laboratory">Guggenheim Aeronautical Laboratory</a> at Caltech (GALCIT), filed two patent applications in Sep 1943 referencing Goddard\'s <span><a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US1102653" rel="nofollow">U.S. Patent 1,102,653</a></span> for the multistage rocket.\n</p><p>By 1939, von K&#225;rm&#225;n\'s GALCIT had received Army Air Corps funding to develop rockets to assist in aircraft take-off. Goddard learned of this in 1940, and openly expressed his displeasure at not being considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burrows_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burrows-77">[77]</a></sup> Malina could not understand why the Army did not arrange for an exchange of information between Goddard and Caltech, since both were under government contract at the same time. Goddard did not think he could be of that much help to Caltech because they were designing rockets with solid fuel, while he was using liquid fuels.\n</p><p>Goddard was concerned with avoiding the public criticism and ridicule he had faced in the 1920s, which he believed had harmed his professional reputation. He also lacked interest in discussions with people who had less understanding of rocketry than he did,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-58"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>171</span></sup> feeling that his time was extremely constrained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-59"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>23</span></sup> Goddard\'s health was frequently poor, as a result of his earlier bout of tuberculosis, and he was uncertain about how long he had to live. He felt, therefore, that he hadn\'t the time to spare arguing with other scientists and the press about his new field of research, or helping all the amateur rocketeers who wrote to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-60"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>61,71,110&#8211;11,114&#8211;15</span></sup> In 1932 Goddard wrote to H. G. Wells:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p> How many more years I shall be able to work on the problem, I do not know; I hope, as long as I live. There can be no thought of finishing, for "aiming at the stars", both literally and figuratively, is a problem to occupy generations, so that no matter how much progress one makes, there is always the thrill of just beginning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoiko_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Stoiko-15">[15]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Goddard spoke to professional groups, published articles and papers and patented his ideas; but while he discussed basic principles, he was unwilling to reveal the details of his designs until he had flown rockets to high altitudes and thus proven his theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-61"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>115</span></sup> He tended to avoid any mention of space flight, and spoke only of high-altitude research, since he believed that other scientists regarded the subject as unscientific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-62"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>116</span></sup>\n</p><p>However, Goddard\'s tendency to secrecy was not absolute, nor was he totally uncooperative. In 1945 GALCIT was building the <a href="/wiki/WAC_Corporal" title="WAC Corporal">WAC Corporal</a> for the Army but was having trouble with the rocket\'s engine performance. Frank Malina went to Annapolis and consulted with Goddard and they arrived at a solution to the liquid propellant problem, which resulted in the successful launch of the high-altitude research rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heinz_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heinz-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the First and Second World Wars, Goddard offered his services, patents and technology to the military, and made some significant contributions. Just before the Second World War several young Army officers, and some higher-ranking ones, believed Goddard\'s research was important but were unable to generate funds for his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glenn_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Toward the end of his life, Goddard, realizing he was no longer going to be able to make significant progress alone in his field, joined the American Rocket Society and became a director. He made plans to work in the budding US aerospace industry (with Curtiss-Wright), taking most of his team with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-63"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>382,385</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>On June 21, 1924, Goddard married Esther Christine Kisk (March 31, 1901 &#8211; June 4, 1982),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> a secretary in Clark University\'s President\'s office, whom he had met in 1919. She became enthusiastic about rocketry and photographed some of his work as well as aided him in his experiments and paperwork, including accounting. They enjoyed going to the movies in Roswell and participated in community organizations such as the Rotary and the Woman\'s Club. He painted the New Mexican scenery, sometimes with artist <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hurd" title="Peter Hurd">Peter Hurd</a>, and played the piano. She played bridge, while he read. Esther said Robert participated in the community, and readily accepted invitations to speak to church and service groups. The couple did not have children. After his death, she sorted out Goddard\'s papers, and secured 131 additional patents on his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Concerning Goddard\'s religious views, he was raised as an <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopalian</a>, though he was not outwardly religious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The Goddards were associated with the Episcopal church in Roswell, and he attended occasionally. He once spoke to a young people\'s group on the relationship of science and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-64"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>224</span></sup>\n</p><p>Goddard\'s serious bout with tuberculosis weakened his lungs, affecting his ability to work, and was one reason he liked to work alone, in order to avoid argument, and confrontation with others and use his time fruitfully. He labored with the prospect of a shorter than average life span. After arriving in Roswell, Goddard applied for life insurance, but when the company doctor examined him he said that Goddard belonged in a bed in Switzerland (where he could get the best care).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-65"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>183</span></sup> Goddard\'s health began to deteriorate further after moving to the humid climate of Maryland to work for the Navy. He was diagnosed with throat cancer in 1945. He continued to work, able to speak only in a whisper, until surgery was required, and he died in August of that year in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore,_Maryland" title="Baltimore, Maryland">Baltimore, Maryland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-66"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>377,395</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> He was buried in <a href="/wiki/Hope_Cemetery_(Worcester,_Massachusetts)" title="Hope Cemetery (Worcester, Massachusetts)">Hope Cemetery</a> in his home town of Worcester, Massachusetts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Robert+H.+Goddard%22" rel="nofollow">"Robert H. Goddard"</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Robert+H.+Goddard%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Robert+H.+Goddard%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Robert+H.+Goddard%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Robert+H.+Goddard%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Robert+H.+Goddard%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li>Goddard was credited with 214 <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patents</a> for his work; 131 of these were awarded after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoddardPatents_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoddardPatents-84">[84]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Goddard influenced several people who went on to do significant work in the U.S. space program, such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Truax" title="Robert Truax">Robert Truax</a> (USN), <a href="/wiki/Milton_Rosen" title="Milton Rosen">Milton Rosen</a> (Naval Research Laboratory and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>), astronauts <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">Jim Lovell</a>, NASA <a href="/wiki/Flight_controller" title="Flight controller">flight controller</a> <a href="/wiki/Gene_Kranz" title="Gene Kranz">Gene Kranz</a>, astrodynamicist <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Herrick_(astronomer)" title="Samuel Herrick (astronomer)">Samuel Herrick</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UCLA" title="UCLA">UCLA</a>), and General Jimmy Doolittle (US Army and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NACA" title="NACA">NACA</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glenn_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn-58">[58]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Goddard received the <a href="/wiki/Langley_Gold_Medal" title="Langley Gold Medal">Langley Gold Medal</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> in 1960, and the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a> in September 16, 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LynnJenner_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-LynnJenner-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">Goddard Space Flight Center</a>, a NASA facility in <a href="/wiki/Greenbelt,_Maryland" title="Greenbelt, Maryland">Greenbelt, Maryland</a>, was established in 1959. The crater <a href="/wiki/Goddard_(crater)" title="Goddard (crater)">Goddard</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> is also named in his honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crater_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crater-85">[85]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The Dr. Robert H. Goddard Collection and the Robert Goddard Exhibition Room are housed in the Archives and Special Collections area of Clark University\'s <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Goddard_Library" title="Robert H. Goddard Library">Robert H. Goddard Library</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Goddard_High_School_(New_Mexico)" title="Goddard High School (New Mexico)">Robert H. Goddard High School</a> was completed in 1965 in <a href="/wiki/Roswell,_New_Mexico" title="Roswell, New Mexico">Roswell, New Mexico</a>, and dedicated by Esther Goddard;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the school\'s mascot is titled "Rockets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RISD_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-RISD-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Robert H. Goddard Middle School is in <a href="/wiki/Glendora,_California" title="Glendora, California">Glendora, California</a>.  Their mascot is the "Titan", but not the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titan</a> of Greek mythology&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(rocket_family)" title="Titan (rocket family)">Titan Rocket</a>.</li>\n<li>A small memorial with a statue of Goddard is located at the site where Goddard launched the first liquid-propelled rocket, now the Pakachoag golf course in Auburn, Massachusetts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>\n<li>Release 13 of the <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> distribution <a href="/wiki/Fedora_(operating_system)" title="Fedora (operating system)">Fedora</a> is named after Goddard.</li>\n<li>The television series <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Next_Generation" title="Star Trek: The Next Generation">Star Trek: The Next Generation</a></i> had a shuttlecraft named after Goddard.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Jimmy_Neutron_characters#Goddard" title="List of Jimmy Neutron characters">robotic dog companion</a> of the titular character in the American computer animated television series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Jimmy_Neutron:_Boy_Genius" title="The Adventures of Jimmy Neutron: Boy Genius">The Adventures of Jimmy Neutron: Boy Genius</a></i> is named after Goddard.</li>\n<li>Goddard is a character in some written works of <a href="/wiki/Alternate_history" title="Alternate history">alternate history</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Harry_Turtledove" title="Harry Turtledove">Harry Turtledove</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Worldwar_series" title="Worldwar series">Worldwar series</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allen_M._Steele" title="Allen M. Steele">Allen M. Steele</a>\'s <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=V-S_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="V-S Day (page does not exist)">V-S Day</a></i>.</li>\n<li>Goddard Ave. in <a href="/wiki/Norman,_Oklahoma" title="Norman, Oklahoma">Norman, Oklahoma</a> is named in his honor.</li>\n<li>Goddard Park in <a href="/wiki/Auburn,_Massachusetts" title="Auburn, Massachusetts">Auburn, Massachusetts</a> is named in his honor, the park has two rockets and is adjacent to the Auburn Public Library.</li>\n<li>Goddard Drive, the main road through <a href="/wiki/Malmstrom_Air_Force_Base" title="Malmstrom Air Force Base">Malmstrom Air Force Base</a>, is named in his honor.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_Goddard" title="Blue Origin Goddard">New Goddard</a> prototype experimental reusable vertical launch and landing rocket from <a href="/wiki/Blue_Origin" title="Blue Origin">Blue Origin</a> is named after Goddard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Bottle Rocket, an ale made by the Wormtown Brewery of Worcester, Massachusetts is named in Robert Goddard\'s honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BottleRocket_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-BottleRocket-88">[88]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r874315073">.mw-parser-output href="/wiki/Goddard_High_School_(New_Mexico)" title="Goddard High School (New Mexico)">Robert H. Goddard High School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roswell,_New_Mexico" title="Roswell, New Mexico">Roswell, New Mexico</a>.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patents_of_interest">Patents of interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Patents of interest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Goddard received 214 <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patents</a> for his work, of which 131 were awarded after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoddardPatents_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-GoddardPatents-84">[84]</a></sup> Among the most influential patents were:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Patent 2,395,113 &#8211; "method for feeding combustion liquids to rocket apparatus" &#8211; R. H. Goddard\'s first patent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoddardPatents_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-GoddardPatents-84">[84]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Patent 2,397,657 &#8211; "control mechanism for a rocket apparatus" with "an outside starting device" &#8211; R. H. Goddard\'s second patent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoddardPatents_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-GoddardPatents-84">[84]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Patent 2,397,659 &#8211; "control mechanism for a rocket apparatus" with "self-starting devices for intermittent operation and seals for fuel pumps" &#8211; R. H. Goddard\'s third patent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoddardPatents_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-GoddardPatents-84">[84]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span><a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US1102653" rel="nofollow">U.S. Patent 1,102,653</a></span> &#8211; <i>Rocket apparatus</i> &#8211; R. H. Goddard</li>\n<li><span><a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US1103503" rel="nofollow">U.S. Patent 1,103,503</a></span> &#8211; <i>Rocket apparatus</i> &#8211; R. H. Goddard</li>\n<li><span><a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US2511979" rel="nofollow">A U.S. Patent 2,511,979 A</a></span> &#8211; <i>Vacuum tube transportation system</i> &#8211; E. C. Goddard</li></ul>\n<p>The Guggenheim Foundation and Goddard\'s estate filed suit in 1951 against the U.S. government for prior infringement of Goddard\'s first three patents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoddardPatents_84-5"><a href="#cite_note-GoddardPatents-84">[84]</a></sup> In 1960, the parties settled the suit, and the U.S. armed forces and NASA paid out an award of $1 million: half of the award settlement went to his wife, Esther. At that time, it was the largest government settlement ever paid in a patent case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoddardPatents_84-6"><a href="#cite_note-GoddardPatents-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-67"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>404</span></sup>  The settlement amount exceeded the total amount of all the funding that Goddard received for his work, throughout his entire career.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_firsts">Other firsts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Other firsts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>First American to explore mathematically the practicality of using rocket propulsion to reach high altitudes and to traject to the moon (1912)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>First to receive a U.S. patent on the idea of a multistage rocket (1914)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>First to prove, by actual static test, that rocket propulsion operates in a vacuum, that it needs no air to push against (1915&#8211;1916)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>First to develop suitable lightweight pumps for liquid-fuel rockets (1923)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>First to develop and successfully fly a liquid-fuel rocket (March 16, 1926)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-4"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>First to launch a scientific payload (a barometer, a thermometer, and a camera) in a rocket flight (1929)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-5"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>First to use vanes in the rocket engine exhaust for guidance (1932)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-6"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>First to develop gyroscopic control apparatus for guiding rocket flight (1932)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-7"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>First to fire a liquid-fuel rocket faster than the speed of sound (1935)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-8"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>First to launch and successfully guide a rocket with an engine pivoted by moving the tail section (as if on gimbals) controlled by a gyro mechanism (1937)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_89-9"><a href="#cite_note-venture-89">[89]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quotations">Quotations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Quotations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-Over-quotation plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Quotations#Specific_recommendations" title="Wikipedia:Quotations">too many or too-lengthy quotations</a> for an encyclopedic entry</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">help improve the article</a> by presenting facts as a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrally-worded</a> summary with <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">appropriate citations</a>. Consider transferring direct quotations to <a href="/wiki/Wikiquote" title="Wikiquote">Wikiquote</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2018</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li>"It is difficult to say what is impossible, for the dream of yesterday is the hope of today and the reality of tomorrow." (From his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_school" title="High school">high school</a> graduation oration, "On Taking Things for Granted", June 1904)</li>\n<li>"On the afternoon of October 19, 1899, I climbed a tall cherry tree and, armed with a saw which I still have, and a hatchet, started to trim the dead limbs from the cherry tree. It was one of the quiet, colorful afternoons of sheer beauty which we have in October in New England, and as I looked towards the fields at the east, I imagined how wonderful it would be to make some device which had even the possibility of ascending to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>. I was a different boy when I descended the tree from when I ascended for existence at last seemed very purposive." (Written later, in an autobiographical sketch)</li>\n<li>"Every vision is a joke until the first man accomplishes it; once realized, it becomes commonplace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_University_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_University-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-43">[43]</a></sup> (His response to a reporter\'s question following criticism in <i>The New York Times</i>, 1920)</li>\n<li>"It is not a simple matter to differentiate unsuccessful from successful experiments. ... [Most] work that is finally successful is the result of a series of unsuccessful tests in which difficulties are gradually eliminated."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_14-68"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>274</span></sup> (Written to a correspondent, early 1940s)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_H._Goddard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-Prose plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Prose" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="40" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>is in a list format
